At  a  Meeting  of  the  Commlttee  of  tlie  Bannatyne  Club,  held  on 
Frlday,  12th  of  Novembcr  1847. 

It  having  been  stated  to  the  Meeting  that,  agreeably  to  a  former  resoUi- 
tion  of  the  Commlttee,  the  Registrum  de  Inchaffery  had  been  sent  to 
press,  from  the  transcript  presented  to  the  Club  by  Henry  Drummond, 
Esq.  of  London,  and  that  the  Right  Honourable  the  Earl  of  Kinnoull 
had  granted  the  use  of  the  orlglnal  Manuscrlpt  preserved  In  the  Library  at 
Dupplln,  for  the  purpose  of  collation,  and  the  volume  being  nearly  ready 
for  dehvery,  the  Committee  accordingly 

Resolved, — 

That  the  Registrum  de  Inchaffery,  when  completod,  be  clrcu- 
lated  amoncr  the  Members  of  the  Club. 


Ejstractedjrom  the  Minutes  of  the  Cluh.

THE  PREFACE. 


The  beautiful  valley  of  Stratherne  has  some  peculiar  points  of  interest 
for  the  Scotch  historlan  and  antiquary.  It  contains,  in  the  forts  scattered 
over  its  lower  district,  and  in  the  remarkable  '  round  tOM^er'  of  Abernethy, 
among  the  oldest  vestiges  of  civil  dominion  and  of  ecclesiastical  antiqui- 
ties  that  remain  to  us.  The  Earldom  ascends  to  a  high  and  romantic 
antiquity,  and  it  ■was  our  only  County  palatine.  Its  great  Earls,  of  the 
ancient  Scotch  blood,  held  their  own,  amid  the  innovations  of  David  I.,  as 
the  leaders  of  the  Celtic  party,  and  supported  their  native  customs  against 
the  new-fangledness  of  the  Saxon  and  Norman  chivalry-*  The  older 
chroniclers  mention  them  with  much  of  the  respect  paid  to  royalty. 

Mahs  was  the  Count  of  that  time,  perhaps  the  first  of  the  race  who 
accepted  the  new  southern  title  of  honour.  He  was  not  a  man  of  parch- 
raents ;  or  at  least  we  have  no  charters  granted  to  hlm  or  by  him.  He 
was  one  of  the  six  Earls  who  attested  or  consented  to  the  re-erection  of 
the  abbey  of  Scone,  by  King  Alexander  I.,  about  the  year  1114 ;  and  hc 
witnessed  another  grant  to  the  same  monastery ;  ^  one  to  the  cathedral  of 
Glasgow,"  and  several  charters  of  David  I.  to  Dunfermhne.    We  know  hira 

'  Ailred  de  Bello  Standardi,  «fcc.  '  Regist.   Glasg.   p.  9.     The  charter  was 

Liber  de  Scon,  N.  1,  3.     The  second      granted  in  1136. 
oharter  is  between  1122  and  1124.

agaln  only  in  the  lively  sketch  of  the  battle  of  the  Standard,  by  Abbot 
Aihed,  (A.D.  1138.)  He  wore  no  arraour,  but  went  to  battle  in  his 
country  fashion.  He  blamed  the  King  for  trusting  so  much  to  the  French- 
men — "Not  one  of  them,  with  all  his  arms,  shall  be  more  forward  in 
battle  this  day  than  I  shall ;"  and  his  taunts  had  nearly  led  to  a  quarrel 
with  Alan  de  Percy,  a  Norman  knight,  a  foUower  of  David;  but  the 
King  interposed. 

We  know  no  more  of  the  next  Earl,  Ferteth  or  Ferquhard.  In  1160 
he  headed  a  conspiracy  of  native  Scots,  irritated  at  King  Malcolm's  English 
counsels.  "We  will  not,"  said  they,  "have  Henry  to  rule  over  us." 
They  assaulted  the  tower  in  which  the  King  had  sought  refuge,  and  though 
repulsed,  were  too  powerful  and  dangerous  to  be  brought  to  punishment.'' 
Earl  Ferteth  witnessed  a  charter  of  King  Malcolm  about  the  year  1155.' 
He  is  mentioned  as  alive  in  a  charter  to  the  abbey  of  Scone,  in  1164,' 
and  he  died  in  1171-^ 

With  Earl  Gilbert,  the  son  of  Ferteth,  we  become  better  acquainted. 
He  adopted  the  Norman  fashions ;  took  charters  for  his  lands  ;  practised  the 
usages  of  knightly  heraldry ;  connected  himself  with  Norman  families  by 
marriage,  and  rivalled  the  most  zealous  followers  of  David  in  his  munifi- 
cence  to  the  church. 

Fordun  tells  us  a  strange  legend, — that  Earl  Gilbert  of  Stratherne 
divided  his  earldom  in  three  equal  parts,  one  for  the  Bishop  of  Dunblane ; 
another  he  bestowed  on  Saint  John  the  Evangelist  and  the  canons  of 
Inchaffray ;  and  reserved  the  third  for  himself  and  his  heirs  in  his  earl- 
dom.''  Though  we  find  no  trace  of  such  extreme  munificence,  it  is  certain 
that  Earl  Gilbert  foUowed  the  fashion  of  the  ase  in  liberal  endowments  to

the  church.  The  family  of  Stratherne  were  the  only  Scotch  subjects  who 
could  claim  the  distinction  of  having  founded  a  bishopric  and  inheriting  its 
patronage,  unless  we  except  the  great  lords  of  Galloway,  who  appcar  to 
have  renewed  the  foundation  of  the  venerable  see  of  St  Ninian.' 

Whether  we  adopt  Fordun's  authority,  and  hold  it  as  certain  that  the  see 
of  Dunblane  was  founded  by  Earl  Gilbert,  who  succeeded  in  1171  and 
died  in  1223,  or  ascribe  its  erection  to  a  somewhat  earlier  pcriod,  it  might 
be  an  inquiry  of  some  interest  to  endeavour  to  ascertain  from  what  dio- 
ceses  the  territory  assigned  to  the  new  see  was  disjoined.  It  is  not  pro- 
bable  that  the  old  bishopric  of  Cumbria  extended  at  any  time  further  to 
the  northward  than  the  hmits  of  the  later  diocese  of  Glasgow,  vvhich  appear 
on  all  sides  to  have  been  marked  out  by  the  ancient  boundaries  of  the 
British  people  of  Strathclyde  and  Reged.  The  see  of  St  Andrews  may 
probably  have  contributed  a  portion  of  its  south-western  territory  to  the 
new  bishopric,  but  it  seems  likely  that  tlie  great  bulk  of  its  jurisdiction 
was  derived  from  the  diocose  of  Dnnkeld.''

We  may  account  for  this  seeming  anomaly,

Very  early  in  tlie  reign  of  William  (1178-80),  Earl  Gilbert  had  a 
cliarter  of  Kinbethach,  to  be  held  to  him  and  his  heirs,  of  the  King  and  his 
heirs,  as  freely  as  he  held  his  earldom  of  Stratherne.''  Among  the  witnesses 
to  that  charter  is  a  person  styled  Gillecohn  Marescald.  A  few  years  later 
(before  1189)  the  King  granted  to  Earl  Gilbert,  Maddyrnin  (Madderty), 
with  all  its  pertinents,  and  with  all  feudal  privileges  and  jurisdictions,  to  be 
held  for  half  a  knighfs  service — but  undcr  a  remarkable  condition — "  that 
no  part  of  the  land  should  ever  be  sold  to  Gillecolme  Marescall,  or  his  heirs, 
or  any  one  of  his  race,  seeing  the  said  Gillecolm  forfeited  that  land  for  felony 
done  against  the  King,  in  that  he  rendered  up  the  KIng's  castle  of  Heryn 
feloniously,  and  afterwards  wickedly  and   traitorously  went  over   to   his 


riers  of  sea  and  land,  by  supposing  that  when

owu  jurisdiction,  either  on  account  of  the

trious  by  so  many  associations,  or  by  reason

mory  of  Saint  Columba  was  had  at  Dunkeld,

appear  that  lona  was  at  any  time  of  old  in-

received  consecratiou,  even  after  they  were

pavliament,  in  the  14th  centurj'.

Dunblane,  Brechin,  Aberdeen,  Moray,  Ross, 
and  Caithness.  (Reffist.  Glasg.  p.  77.)  Lis- 
more,  or  Argyll,  had  not  yet  been  separated 
from  Dunkeld.  Galloway  was  then  acknow- 
ledged  to  be  suiFragan  of  York  ;  and  Orkney 
and  the  Isles  were,untillong  afterwards,  in  the

of  St  Patrick  of  Strogeth,  of  St  Mechesseok  of  Ochterardouer,  of  St 
Beanus  of  Kynkell ;  with  the  tithe  of  the  Earl's  cain  and  rents  of  wheat, 
meal,  mah,  cheese,  and  all  provisions  used  throughout  the  year  in  his  court ; 
with  tithe  of  all  fish  brought  into  his  kitchen,  and  of  the  produce  of  his 
hunting ;  and  the  tithe  of  all  the  profits  of  his  courts  of  justice,  and  all 
offerings.  The  convent  had  the  hberty  of  fishing  in  the  Peffer,  and  of 
fishing  and  birding  over  all  the  Earl's  lands,  Avaters,  and  lakes.  They 
might  take  timber,  for  building  and  all  other  uses,  firom  his  woods,  and 
have  their  pannage,  or  mast  feeding  for  pigs,  as  well  as  bark  and  fire- 
wood,  in  whatever  places,  and  as  much  as,  they  chose.  Some  years  later, 
Earl  Gilbert  granted  to  the  canons,  now  seated  at  Inchafiray,  the  church 
of  St  Beanus  of  Foulis,  with  the  'dower'  land  of  the  church,  and  the

them  the  churches  of  Gask  and  Strogeth.

The  family  of  Stratherne,  and  its  possessions,  have  been  so  mixed  up 
witb  the  romantic  events  of  Scotch  history,  that  they  have  been  naturally 
subjected  to  some  exaggeration.  The  ancient  earldom  has  been  described 
as  including  "the  haill  lands  lying  betwixt  Croce  Macduff  at  Newburgh, 
and  the  west  end  of  Balquhidder,  in  length ;  the  Oichell  hills  and  the  hills 
called  Montes  Grampii,  in  breadth." '  This  Register,  and  the  charters 
which  accompany  it,  show  that  there  were  many  independent  lords  within 
that  district  from  the  earhest  period  of  record,  and  almost  as  early  as  the 
earldom  can  have  been  held,  at  least  by  that  title.  We  even  become 
acquainted  with  a  Royal  castle  of  '  Earn,'  that  strength  betrayed  by  the

The  cathedral  of  St  Blane,  originally  founded  and  endowed  by  the  Earls 
of  Stratherne,  continued  under  their  protection  until  the  earldom  had 
merged  in  the  crown,  and  the  bishop  and  chapter  held  their  lands,  annual 
rents,  and  temporahtics,  of  the  earls,  as  their  feudal  superiors.  In  1442 
James  II.,  in  Parhament,  declared  the  earldom  fallen  to  the  crown,  and 
ordained  the  bishopric  temporalities  henceforth  to  be  held  in  free  barony 
directly  of  the  sovereign.''

The  abbey  of  Inchaffray,  though  respectably  endowed,  does  not  seem 
to  have  ranked  among  the  greater  monasteries  of  Scotland.  The  abbots, 
though  prelates  of  parliament,  occur  rarely  in  pubUc  affairs,  or  in  the 
transactions  which  so  frequently  broaght  together  churchmen  of  various 
reUgious  houses.  We  have  thus  only  a  very  few  names  of  the  successive 
abbots  preserved. 

MaUs,  a  reUgious  hermit,  was  the  person  to  whom  Earl  Gilbert  committed 
the  selection  of  the  convent  at  its  first  foundation  in  1200,  and  he  was  the 
first  head  of  the  house." 

Innocentius  appears  to  have  been  head  of  the  house  as  Prior,  and  was 
perhaps  thc  first  who  took  the  style  of  Abbot,  in  the  time  of  Earl  Robert, 
between  1223  and  1231.*^ 

Alanus  occurs  as  Abbot  of  InchaSray,  from  1258  tiU  1271.® 

Hugh,  who  had  been  prior  of  the  convent,  was  afterwards  Abbot  in  1284.'' 

Maurice  was  the  Abbot  of  Inchaffray  who  blessed  the  army  of  Bruce  at 
Bannockburn  (1314,  June  24.)'  He  was  promoted  to  the  see  of  his  own 
diocese  of  Dunblane  in  1319.''

and  he  haunted  court  too  much,  and  had  now  purchased  to  be  one  of  the 
Session  and  Privy  Council,  which  cannot  agree  with  the  office  of  a  pastor  or 
bishop ;  that  he  had  resigned  Inchaffray  in  favour  of  a  young  child,  and 
set  diverse  lands  in  feu,  in  prejudice  of  the  kirk."  The  Bishop  of  Galloway 
"granted  that  he  offended  in  all  that  was  laid  to  his  charge.'" 

The  youth  in  whose  favour  he  had  resigned  the  Abbacy  of  Inchaffray, 
was  James  Drummond  of  Inverpeffray,  the  second  son  of  David,  second 
Lord  Drummond,  who  was  commendator  of  Inchaffray  on  the  13th  of  March 
1556,  when  David  Lord  Drummond  acted  with  him  as  his  coadjutor." 
The  abbacy  of  Inchaffray  was  erected  into  a  temporal  lordship  in  his 
favour,  and  he  was  created  Lord  Madcrtie  in  1609.  From  him  is  descended 
the  noble  family  of  Strathallan. 

The  ancient  Reglster  of  the  abbey  of  Inchaffray  has  been  for  some  time 
preserved   in   tho  hbrary  at   Duplin  Castle.      It  was   referred   to  by  the

\ 

narrative  of  tlie  Papal  commission  proceeds  partly  upon  liis  intbrmation. 
It  sets  forth  tbat  tlie  -see  had  been  vacant  for  above  te»  j-ears,  during 
which  time  its  property  had  been  plundered,  so  that  no  fit  persou  could 
be  induced  to  hold  the  office,  until  the  Papal  commissioners  had  fixed 
upon  the  then  Bishop,  in  the  hope  that  through  him  the  church  might 
breathe  again  out  of  its  slough  of  misery — that  the  new  bishop  had  found  it 
so  desolatc  that  he  had  not  where  to  lay  liis  head  in  the  cathedrah  There 
was  no  chapter ;  but  in  the  unroofed  church,  a  certain  rustic  chaplain 
performed  divine  servicej  while  the  bishop's  revenues  were  so  slender,  they 
scai-ce  afforded  fitting  maintenance  for  half  tlie  year.  Upon  this  statement, 
the  Popc  granted  commission  for  assigning  to  the  Bishop,  the  fourth  of  the 
tithes  of  the  whole  parishes  of  the  diocese,  from  which  he  was  to  reserve  a 
fitting  sustenance  for  himseh  and  to  provide  for  a  dean  and  canons  to  be 
estabhshed  by  the  Papal  commissloners.  Or  otherwise,  the  commissioners 
were  to  assign  the  fourth  of  the  tithes  to  the  Bishop ;  to  transfer  the  epis- 
copal  sec  to  the  monastery  of  canons  rcgular  of  St  John  (of  Inchaffray ;) 
and  to  constitutc  thc  canons  the  electoral  cliapter  of  the  diocese. 

It  is  certain  that  the  alternative  of  raising  the  convent  of  Inchaffray  into 
the  chapter  of  the  diocese  did  not  take  cffcct ;  and  the  cathedral  continued 
to  be  governed  by  a  secular  chapter.  The  Papal  comraission,  contrary  to  its 
avowed  purpose,  produced  an  abandonment  by  the  Bishop  of  all  right  of 
pension  out  of  the  lands  or  churches  of  the  Earl  of  Menteth,  and  a  per- 
mission  to  the  Earl  to  found  a  house  of  Augustinian  canons  in  the  Isle  of 
Inchmahomok,  with  the  churches  of  Lany  and  of  the  isle  itself  for  their 
endowment.  The  church  of  Kippen  was  assigned  by  the  Earl  to  form  a 
canonry  in  the  cathedral,  and  he  made  over  to  the  Bishop  all  right  to  the 
church  of  Callendar.

from  rccords  preserved  in  the  Cleneral  llegister  House  at  Edinburgh.

The  first  part  of  the  foiirth  consists  of  an  abbreviate  of  original  charters 
from  the  charter-chests  of  Anthony  Murray  of  Creiff,  Esq.,  and  of  Alex- 
ander  Anderson  Henry,  Esq.  of  Woodend,  to  whom  the  Club  is  indebted 
for  their  use.  The  remainder  of  thc  fourth  Appendix  is  an  abbrevl- 
ation  of  charters,  from  a  transcript  of  the  originals  preserved  in  the  Ubrary 
at  Innerpeffry,  a  foundation  of  David  Lord  Madertie.  The  transcript 
is  the  property  of  the  Maitland  Club,  to  whose  members,  as  well  as  to  the 
attentive  courtesy  of  Professor  Fleming  of  Glasgow,  the  Bannatyne  Club 
is  indebted  for  its  use. 

It  appears  that  as  early  as  the  year  1218,  the  canons  of  Inchafiray  had 
reclaimed  a  portion  of  the  vast  marsh  in  whlch  their  'isle  of  masses'  stood." 
Nearly  five  centurles  afterwards,  the  "  heritors,  upon  the  pow  of  Inchaf- 
fray,"  appHed  to  parhament  to  appolnt  commlssioners  for  dralning  the  whole 
marsh  for  common  benefit.  The  act  which  followed  upon  their  petltion  Is 
the  fifth  artlcle  of  the  Appendix.  The  date,  which  is  erroneously  printed 
in  the  title,  is  correctly  glven  in  the  body  of  the  Act — October  9th,  1696.'' 
It  Is  curious  as  perhaps  the  slngle  instance  of  a  great  agrlcultiiral  Improve- 
ment  efibcted  under  the  authority  of  the  Scotch  parliament. 

Our  heralds  tell  us,  "  the  old  Earls  of  Stratherne  carried  for  arms  Or, 
two  cheverons  ^w/e^ :"  and  undoubtedly  the  Earls  Malis  of  the  13th  century 
bore  that  coat,  as  did  also  their  vassals  (perhaps  too  thelr  kinsmen)  the 
family  of  the  Seneschals  of  Stratherne,  from  whom  the  house  of  Tullbardln 
is  descended.  On  a  seal  appended  to  a  charter  of  Mahs  Earl  of  Stratherne, 
(prlnted  In  the  Appendix  to  the  Preface,  No.  19)  the  double  cheveron  is  seen 
both  on  the  shield  and  over  all  the  housings  of  the  horse.     The  pretty  seal

V. 


ACT  IN  FAVORS  OF  SIR  ROBERT  MURRAY  OF 
ABERCAIRNIE  AND  OTHER  HERITORS  UPON  THE 
POW  OF  INCHAFFRAY.     OCTOBER  9,  1690. 


AT  EDINBURGH  the  ninth  day  of  October  one  thousand  six  hundred  and  ninety 
six  years  Anent  the  Petition  given  in  and  presented  to  His  Majesties  High  Commis- 
sioner  and  the  Honourable  Estates  of  Parliament  by  Sir  Robert  Murray  of  Aber- 
cairnie  Sir  Patricls  Murray  of  Auchtertyre  Mungo  Graham  of  Gorthie  and  James 
Ohphant  of  Wilhamstoun  Heritors  upon  the  Pow  of  InchafFray,  Humbly  Shewing
